            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Drury, 22, was one of the pieces the Diamondbacks acquired in the Justin Upton deal. Primarily a third baseman, Drury has dabbled at second base and shortstop. For a team like Arizona that is lacking in quality infield depth, Drury could have some type of breakout season if given the playing time. He hit .331/.384/.458 with Triple-A Reno in 2015, but his over-the-fence power has almost disappeared. He hit 15 home runs in the Midwest League in 2013 and 23 home runs across two levels in 2014. In 2015, he hit just five home runs in the minors. He was able to hit two with the Diamondbacks, so his power numbers will be something to watch going forward. The ability to play multiple positions puts him in a better spot to succeed as a major leaguer, but he might not be the ideal fantasy option. Arizona has decent infield options, but second base appears to be lacking most on the offensive side of the ball. If he hits enough, it wouldn't be a surprise to he him supplant Chris Owings at second base, provided Nick Ahmed and Jean Segura share shortstop. He could also be the first one to step in if Jake Lamb struggles or gets injured. (Dustin Nosler)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Drury made his major league debut and posted a .214/.254/.375 triple slash in 59 plate appearances. Five of the infielder's 12 hits went for extra bases.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>An intriguing prospect heading into 2016, Brandon Drury may have earned himself a starting role moving forward for the Arizona Diamondbacks. In terms of the total body of work, Drury hit an impressive .282 and reached base at a .329 clip in his first full big league season. An aggressive hitter, Drury walked just 6.2% of the time to go along with an even 20% strikeout rate. June and July brought some struggles for him in his rookie campaign, but he recovered and went for a .357 batting average in September to close the season. His park-adjusted offense had him two percentage points above league average for the year, a mark which came in 13% above league average in the season's second half. The power is likely there, as his isolated power came in at .176 thanks to 16 home runs and 31 doubles. The Diamondbacks may work with him on his approach in order to drive that walk rate up a bit and try and get him to cut down on strikeouts. However, in maintaining a 79% contact rate in his first full season and flashing the type of power that he did, a sophomore campaign in which he's fully adjusted to big league pitching could be an extremely positive one. His versatility alone makes him an intriguing quantity, especially if he provides the same kind of power. (Randy Holt)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>With a strong showing in the spring, Brandon Drury forced his way onto the Diamondbacks roster, not disappointing with a .282 batting average and .329 on-base percentage in his rookie season. If he can improve his approach (6.2% walk rate) and cut down on strikeouts, Drury could be a major asset in the power game for Arizona in 2017, as indicated by his .176 ISO.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

But Drury took tiny steps back pretty much across the board in his sophomore season. His plate discipline, contact, hard contact, and power numbers were all a bit lower in 2017 than in 2016, and even his 2016 production looked more like a bench player’s than a future everyday player’s. Between Ketel Marte, Chris Owings, and Drury, the Diamondbacks seem to have three players for the two spots in their middle infield. That traffic jam may not actually be an issue, especially if the team opts to use Owings as a super-utility player, which they did last year. Owings saw between 15 and 51 starts at four different positions, and that left Drury with 109 starts at second base. But if Drury can’t develop a plus tool or at least raise the sum of all of his tools to the level of an average player, I worry that the Diamondbacks will prefer to offer Owings the stability of an everyday position at Drury’s expense.
